<p>The <b>snow leopard</b> (<i><b>Panthera uncia</b></i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_name" title="Common name">commonly</a> known as the <b>ounce</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of large cat in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Panthera" title="Panthera">Panthera</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">Felidae</a>. The species is native to the mountain ranges of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>. It is listed as <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a> on the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a> because the global population is estimated to number fewer than 10,000 mature individuals and is expected to decline about 10% by 2040. It is mainly threatened by <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">poaching</a> and <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a> following infrastructural developments. It inhabits <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subalpine_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Subalpine zone">subalpine zones</a> at elevations of 3,000–4,500&#160;m (9,800–14,800&#160;ft), ranging from eastern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a> to southern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_China" title="Western China">western China</a>. In the northern part of its range, it also lives at lower elevations.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">Taxonomically</a>, the snow leopard was long classified in the <a href="/wiki/Monotypic_genus" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotypic genus">monotypic genus</a> <i>Uncia</i>. Since <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> studies revealed the relationships among <i>Panthera</i> species, it has since been considered a member of that <a href="/wiki/Genus_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Genus (biology)">genus</a>. Two subspecies were described based on <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> differences, but <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> differences between the two have not yet been confirmed. It is therefore regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Monotypic_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotypic species">monotypic species</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_and_etymology">Naming and etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ounce1.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ounce1.jpg/220px-Ounce1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ounce1.jpg/330px-Ounce1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Ounce1.jpg/440px-Ounce1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="381" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ounce1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of an 'Ounce'</div></div></div>
<p>Both the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> name <i>uncia</i> and the English word <i>ounce</i> are derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>once</i>, which was to be used for the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_lynx" title="Eurasian lynx">Eurasian lynx</a> (<i>Lynx lynx</i>). <i>Once</i> is thought to have evolved from an earlier variant of <i>lynx</i> by <a href="/wiki/False_splitting" class="mw-redirect" title="False splitting">false splitting</a>; <i>lonce</i> was interpreted as <i>l'once</i>, in which <i>l<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span></i> is the <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">elided form</a> of the French definite article <i>la</i> ('the'), leaving <i>once</i> to be perceived as the animal's name.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
The word <i>panther</i> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">classical Latin</a> <i>panthēra</i>, itself from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">ancient Greek</a> πάνθηρ <i>pánthēr</i>, which was used for spotted cats.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_evolution">Taxonomy and evolution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Panthera_uncia_02_MWNH_355.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Panthera_uncia_02_MWNH_355.jpg/220px-Panthera_uncia_02_MWNH_355.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Panthera_uncia_02_MWNH_355.jpg/330px-Panthera_uncia_02_MWNH_355.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Panthera_uncia_02_MWNH_355.jpg/440px-Panthera_uncia_02_MWNH_355.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Panthera_uncia_02_MWNH_355.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Snow leopard <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a></div></div></div>
<p><i>Felis uncia</i> was the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific name">scientific name</a> used by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christian_Daniel_von_Schreber" title="Johann Christian Daniel von Schreber">Johann Christian Daniel von Schreber</a> in 1777 who <a href="/wiki/Scientific_description" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific description">described</a> a snow leopard based on an earlier description by <a href="/wiki/Georges-Louis_Leclerc,_Comte_de_Buffon" title="Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon">Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon</a>, supposing that the cat occurred along the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_Coast" title="Barbary Coast">Barbary Coast</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_India" title="East India">East India</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Genus_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Genus (biology)">genus</a> name <i>Uncia</i> was proposed by <a href="/wiki/John_Edward_Gray" title="John Edward Gray">John Edward Gray</a> in 1854 for Asian cats with a long and thick tail.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
<i>Felis irbis</i> proposed by <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottfried_Ehrenberg" title="Christian Gottfried Ehrenberg">Christian Gottfried Ehrenberg</a> in 1830 was a skin of a female snow leopard collected in the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a>. He also clarified that several <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopard</a> (<i>P. pardus</i>) skins were previously misidentified as snow leopard skins.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
<i>Felis uncioides</i> proposed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Horsfield" title="Thomas Horsfield">Thomas Horsfield</a> in 1855 was a snow leopard skin from Nepal in the collection of the Museum of the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Uncia uncia</i> was used by <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Innes_Pocock" title="Reginald Innes Pocock">Reginald Innes Pocock</a> in 1930 when he reviewed skins and skulls of <i>Panthera</i> species from Asia. He also described morphological differences between snow leopard and leopard skins.<sup id="cite_ref-Pocock1930_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pocock1930-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
<i>Panthera baikalensis-romanii</i> proposed by a Russian scientist in 2000 was a dark brown snow leopard skin from the <a href="/wiki/Petrovsk-Zabaykalsky_District" title="Petrovsk-Zabaykalsky District">Petrovsk-Zabaykalsky District</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Transbaikal" title="Transbaikal">Transbaikal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The snow leopard was long classified in the <a href="/wiki/Monotypic_genus" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotypic genus">monotypic genus</a> <i>Uncia</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-msw3_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msw3-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
It was subordinated to the genus <i>Panthera</i> based on results of <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogenetic">phylogenetic</a> studies.<sup id="cite_ref-johnson2006_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson2006-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davies2010_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2010-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kitchener2016_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitchener2016-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-catsg_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-catsg-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until spring 2017, there was no evidence available for the recognition of <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>. Results of a <a href="/wiki/Phylogeography" title="Phylogeography">phylogeographic</a> analysis indicate that three subspecies should be recognised:<sup id="cite_ref-Janecka2017_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janecka2017-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<ul><li><i>P. u. uncia</i> in the range countries of the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a></li>
<li><i>P. u. irbis</i> in Mongolia, and</li>
<li><i>P. u. uncioides</i> in the Himalayas and <a href="/wiki/Qinghai" title="Qinghai">Qinghai</a>.</li></ul>
<p>This view has been both contested and supported by different researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, an extinct subspecies <i><a href="/wiki/Panthera_uncia_pyrenaica" title="Panthera uncia pyrenaica">Panthera uncia pyrenaica</a></i> was described in 2022 based on material found in France.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Two_cladograms_for_Panthera.svg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Two_cladograms_for_Panthera.svg/210px-Two_cladograms_for_Panthera.svg.png" decoding="async" width="210" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Two_cladograms_for_Panthera.svg/315px-Two_cladograms_for_Panthera.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Two_cladograms_for_Panthera.svg/420px-Two_cladograms_for_Panthera.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="646" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Two_cladograms_for_Panthera.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two cladograms proposed for <i>Panthera</i>. The upper cladogram is based on two studies published in 2006 and 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-johnson2006_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson2006-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-werdelin2009_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-werdelin2009-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> the lower one is based on studies published in 2010 and 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies2010_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2010-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Based on phylogenetic analysis of <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequence" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA sequence">DNA sequence</a> sampled across the living <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">Felidae</a>, the snow leopard forms a <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister group</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Tiger" title="Tiger">tiger</a> (<i>P. tigris</i>). <a href="/wiki/Genetic_divergence" title="Genetic divergence">Genetic divergence</a> time of this group is estimated at <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=4.62–1.82">4.62&#160;to&#160;1.82</a> million years ago</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-johnson2006_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson2006-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-werdelin2009_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-werdelin2009-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The snow leopard and the tiger probably diverged between <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=3.7–2.7">3.7&#160;to&#160;2.7</a> million years ago</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies2010_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2010-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <i>Panthera</i> originates most likely in northern Central Asia. <i><a href="/wiki/Panthera_blytheae" title="Panthera blytheae">Panthera blytheae</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Excavation_(archaeology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Excavation (archaeology)">excavated</a> in western Tibet's <a href="/wiki/Ngari_Prefecture" title="Ngari Prefecture">Ngari Prefecture</a> is the oldest known <i>Panthera</i> species and exhibits skull characteristics similar to the snow leopard.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_genome" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitochondrial genome">mitochondrial genomes</a> of the snow leopard, the leopard and the <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a> (<i>P. leo</i>) are more similar to each other than their <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_genome" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear genome">nuclear genomes</a>, indicating that their ancestors <a href="/wiki/Hybridization_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hybridization (biology)">hybridised</a> at some point in their <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Panthera_uncia_(33172899150).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Panthera_uncia_%2833172899150%29.jpg/200px-Panthera_uncia_%2833172899150%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="140" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Panthera_uncia_%2833172899150%29.jpg/300px-Panthera_uncia_%2833172899150%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Panthera_uncia_%2833172899150%29.jpg/400px-Panthera_uncia_%2833172899150%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4411" data-file-height="3092" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Showing canines</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:L%C3%A9opard_des_neiges_14081.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/L%C3%A9opard_des_neiges_14081.jpg/200px-L%C3%A9opard_des_neiges_14081.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="288" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/L%C3%A9opard_des_neiges_14081.jpg/300px-L%C3%A9opard_des_neiges_14081.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/L%C3%A9opard_des_neiges_14081.jpg/400px-L%C3%A9opard_des_neiges_14081.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2010" data-file-height="2892" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Thickly furred tail</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The snow leopard's fur is whitish to grey with black spots on the head and neck, with larger <a href="/wiki/Rosette_(zoology)" title="Rosette (zoology)">rosettes</a> on the back, <a href="/wiki/Flank_(anatomy)" title="Flank (anatomy)">flanks</a> and bushy tail. The underbelly is whitish. Its eyes are pale green or grey in color. Its <a href="/wiki/Muzzle_(anatomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Muzzle (anatomy)">muzzle</a> is short and its forehead domed. Its <a href="/wiki/Nasal_cavity" title="Nasal cavity">nasal cavities</a> are large. The fur is thick with hairs between 5 and 12&#160;cm (2.0 and 4.7&#160;in) long. It is stocky, short-legged, and slightly smaller than other cats of the genus <i>Panthera</i>, reaching a shoulder height of 56&#160;cm (22&#160;in), and ranging in head to body size from 75 to 150&#160;cm (30 to 59&#160;in). Its tail is 80 to 105&#160;cm (31 to 41&#160;in) long.<sup id="cite_ref-Hemmer1972_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hemmer1972-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Males average 45 to 55&#160;kg (99 to 121&#160;lb), and females 35 to 40&#160;kg (77 to 88&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Nowell&amp;Jackson1996_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowell&amp;Jackson1996-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, large males reaching 75&#160;kg (165&#160;lb) have been recorded, and small females under 25&#160;kg (55&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-WCoW_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
Its <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canine teeth</a> are 28.6&#160;mm (1.13&#160;in) long and are more slender than those of the other <i>Panthera</i> species.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
In relation to the length of its <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> and width of its <a href="/wiki/Palate" title="Palate">palate</a>, it has large nasal openings, which allow for increasing the volume of air inhaled with each breath, and at the same time for warming and humidifying cold dry air.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> It is not especially adapted to <a href="/wiki/High-altitude_hypoxia" class="mw-redirect" title="High-altitude hypoxia">high-altitude hypoxia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The snow leopard shows several adaptations for living in a cold, mountainous environments. Its small rounded ears help to minimize heat loss. Its broad paws well distribute the body weight for walking on snow, and have fur on their undersides to enhance the grip on steep and unstable surfaces; it also helps to minimize heat loss. Its long and flexible tail helps to balance the cat in the rocky terrain. The tail is very thick due to fat storage, and is covered in a thick layer of fur, which allows the cat to use it like a blanket to protect its face when asleep.<sup id="cite_ref-NatGeog_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeog-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The snow leopard differs from the other <i>Panthera</i> species by a shorter muzzle, an elevated <a href="/wiki/Forehead" title="Forehead">forehead</a>, a vertical chin and a less developed posterior process of the lower <a href="/wiki/Jaw" title="Jaw">jaw</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pocock1930_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pocock1930-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It cannot <a href="/wiki/Roar_(animal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Roar (animal)">roar</a> despite its partly <a href="/wiki/Ossification" title="Ossification">ossified</a> <a href="/wiki/Hyoid_bone" title="Hyoid bone">hyoid bone</a>, as its 9&#160;mm (0.35&#160;in) short <a href="/wiki/Vocal_fold" class="mw-redirect" title="Vocal fold">vocal folds</a> provide little resistance to airflow.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span></h2>
<p>The snow leopard is distributed from the west of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> through southern Siberia, in the <a href="/wiki/Kunlun_Mountains" title="Kunlun Mountains">Kunlun Mountains</a>, Altai Mountains, <a href="/wiki/Sayan_Mountains" title="Sayan Mountains">Sayan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tannu-Ola_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Tannu-Ola Mountains">Tannu-Ola Mountains</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> in eastern Afghanistan, <a href="/wiki/Karakoram" title="Karakoram">Karakoram</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a>, the Tibetan Plateau and in the high elevations of the <a href="/wiki/Himalaya" class="mw-redirect" title="Himalaya">Himalayas</a> in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>. In Mongolia, it inhabits the Mongolian and Gobi Altai Mountains and the <a href="/wiki/Khangai_Mountains" title="Khangai Mountains">Khangai Mountains</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, it occurs up to the <a href="/wiki/Altyn-Tagh" title="Altyn-Tagh">Altyn-Tagh</a> in the north.<sup id="cite_ref-Heptner1972_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heptner1972-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy2003_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy2003-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
It inhabits <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subalpine_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Subalpine zone">subalpine zones</a> at elevations of 3,000 to 4,500&#160;m (9,800 to 14,800&#160;ft), but also lives at lower elevations in the northern part of its range.<sup id="cite_ref-Janecka2008_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janecka2008-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
Potential snow leopard habitat in the Indian Himalayas is estimated at less than 90,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (35,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) in <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir_(union_territory)" title="Jammu and Kashmir (union territory)">Jammu and Kashmir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arunachal_Pradesh" title="Arunachal Pradesh">Arunachal Pradesh</a>, of which about 34,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (13,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) is considered good habitat, and 14.4% is protected. In the beginning of the 1990s, the Indian snow leopard population was estimated at 200–600 individuals living across about 25 protected areas.<sup id="cite_ref-McCarthy2003_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy2003-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In summer, the snow leopard usually lives above the <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a> on <a href="/wiki/Alpine_meadow" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpine meadow">alpine meadows</a> and in rocky regions at elevations of 2,700 to 6,000&#160;m (8,900 to 19,700&#160;ft). In winter, it descends to elevations around 1,200 to 2,000&#160;m (3,900 to 6,600&#160;ft). It prefers rocky, broken terrain, and can move in 85&#160;cm (33&#160;in) deep snow, but prefers to use existing trails made by other animals.<sup id="cite_ref-WCoW_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Snow leopards were recorded by <a href="/wiki/Camera_trap" title="Camera trap">camera traps</a> at 16 locations in northeastern Afghanistan's isolated <a href="/wiki/Wakhan_Corridor" title="Wakhan Corridor">Wakhan Corridor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior_and_ecology">Behavior and ecology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Snow_Leopard_Being_Cute_(16135561540).jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Snow_Leopard_Being_Cute_%2816135561540%29.jpg/170px-Snow_Leopard_Being_Cute_%2816135561540%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Snow_Leopard_Being_Cute_%2816135561540%29.jpg/255px-Snow_Leopard_Being_Cute_%2816135561540%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Snow_Leopard_Being_Cute_%2816135561540%29.jpg/340px-Snow_Leopard_Being_Cute_%2816135561540%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6988" data-file-height="4664" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Snow_Leopard_Being_Cute_(16135561540).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scent_rubbing" title="Scent rubbing">Scent rubbing</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uncia_uncia.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Uncia_uncia.jpg/170px-Uncia_uncia.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Uncia_uncia.jpg/255px-Uncia_uncia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Uncia_uncia.jpg/340px-Uncia_uncia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Uncia_uncia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walking on snow</div></div></div>
<p>The snow leopard's vocalizations include meowing, grunting, <a href="/wiki/Prusten" title="Prusten">prusten</a> and moaning. It can purr when exhaling.<sup id="cite_ref-Hemmer1972_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hemmer1972-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is <a href="/wiki/Solitary_animal" class="mw-redirect" title="Solitary animal">solitary</a> and mostly active at dawn till early morning, and again in afternoons and early evenings. It mostly rests near cliffs and ridges that provide vantage points and shade. In Nepal's <a href="/wiki/Shey_Phoksundo_National_Park" title="Shey Phoksundo National Park">Shey Phoksundo National Park</a>, the home ranges of five adult <a href="/wiki/Radio_telemetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio telemetry">radio-collared</a> snow leopards largely overlapped, though they rarely met. Their individual home ranges ranged from 12 to 39&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (4.6 to 15.1&#160;sq&#160;mi). Males moved between 0.5 and 5.45&#160;km (0.31 and 3.39&#160;mi) per day, and females between 0.2 and 2.25&#160;km (0.12 and 1.40&#160;mi), measured in straight lines between survey points. Since they often zigzagged in the precipitous terrain, they actually moved up to 7&#160;km (4.3&#160;mi) in a single night.<sup id="cite_ref-JacksonAhlborn1988_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JacksonAhlborn1988-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
Up to 10 individuals inhabit an area of 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (40&#160;sq&#160;mi); in habitats with sparse prey, an area of 1,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (400&#160;sq&#160;mi) usually supports only five individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackson1996_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson1996-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A study in the <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Desert" title="Gobi Desert">Gobi Desert</a> from 2008 to 2014 revealed that adult males used a mean home range of 144–270&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (56–104&#160;sq&#160;mi), while adult females ranged in areas of 83–165&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (32–64&#160;sq&#160;mi). Their home ranges overlapped less than 20%. These results indicate that about 40% of the 170 protected areas in their range countries are smaller than the home range of a single male snow leopard.<sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Snow leopards leave <a href="/wiki/Scent_mark" class="mw-redirect" title="Scent mark">scent marks</a> to indicate their territories and common travel routes. They scrape the ground with the hind feet before depositing <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a>, but also <a href="/wiki/Spraying_(animal_behavior)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spraying (animal behavior)">spray</a> urine onto rocks.<sup id="cite_ref-WCoW_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Their urine contains many characteristic low molecular weight compounds with diverse functional groups including <a href="/wiki/Pentanol" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentanol">pentanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hexanol" title="Hexanol">hexanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heptanol" title="Heptanol">heptanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/3-octanone" class="mw-redirect" title="3-octanone">3-octanone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nonanal" title="Nonanal">nonanal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indole" title="Indole">indole</a>, which possibly play a role in chemical communication.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunting_and_diet">Hunting and diet</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Wild_Snow_Leopard_Goes_Grocery_Shopping.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Wild_Snow_Leopard_Goes_Grocery_Shopping.png/200px-Wild_Snow_Leopard_Goes_Grocery_Shopping.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="199" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Wild_Snow_Leopard_Goes_Grocery_Shopping.png/300px-Wild_Snow_Leopard_Goes_Grocery_Shopping.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Wild_Snow_Leopard_Goes_Grocery_Shopping.png/400px-Wild_Snow_Leopard_Goes_Grocery_Shopping.png 2x" data-file-width="836" data-file-height="832" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Snow leopard with a <a href="/wiki/Gray_marmot" title="Gray marmot">gray marmot</a> in Kyrgyzstan</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Hemis_National_Park" title="Hemis National Park">Hemis National Park</a>, a snow leopard was observed approaching prey from above, using rocky cliffs for cover; at a distance of about 40&#160;m (130&#160;ft) from the prey, it walked rapidly for about 15&#160;m (49&#160;ft), ran the last 25&#160;m (82&#160;ft) and killed the prey with a bite to the neck. While squatting on its haunches, it ripped out clumps of hair from the <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a> and then opened it to first feed on the <a href="/wiki/Viscera" class="mw-redirect" title="Viscera">viscera</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
The snow leopard is a <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivore</a> and actively <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunts</a> its <a href="/wiki/Prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Prey">prey</a>. Its preferred wild prey species are <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_blue_sheep" class="mw-redirect" title="Himalayan blue sheep">Himalayan blue sheep</a> (<i>Pseudois nayaur</i>), <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_tahr" title="Himalayan tahr">Himalayan tahr</a> (<i>Hemitragus jemlahicus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Argali" title="Argali">argali</a> (<i>Ovis ammon</i>), <a href="/wiki/Markhor" title="Markhor">markhor</a> (<i>Capra falconeri</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Wild_goat" title="Wild goat">wild goat</a> (<i>C. aegagrus</i>). It also preys on domestic livestock.<sup id="cite_ref-Johansson_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> It prefers prey ranging in weight from 36 to 76&#160;kg (79 to 168&#160;lb), but also hunts smaller mammals such as <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_marmot" title="Himalayan marmot">Himalayan marmot</a> (<i>Marmota himalayana</i>), <a href="/wiki/Pika" title="Pika">pika</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">vole</a> species. Its diet depends on prey availability and varies across its range and season. In the Himalayas, it preys mostly on Himalayan blue sheep, <a href="/wiki/Siberian_ibex" title="Siberian ibex">Siberian ibex</a> (<i>C. sibirica</i>), <a href="/wiki/White-bellied_musk_deer" title="White-bellied musk deer">white-bellied musk deer</a> (<i>Moschus leucogaster</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boar</a> (<i>Sus scrofa</i>). In the <a href="/wiki/Karakoram" title="Karakoram">Karakoram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a>, Altai and Mongolia's Tost Mountains, its main prey consists of Siberian ibex, <a href="/wiki/Thorold%27s_deer" title="Thorold&#39;s deer">Thorold's deer</a> (<i>Cervus albirostris</i>), <a href="/wiki/Siberian_roe_deer" title="Siberian roe deer">Siberian roe deer</a> (<i>Capreolus pygargus</i>) and argali.<sup id="cite_ref-Lyngdoh2014_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lyngdoh2014-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shehzad2012_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shehzad2012-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Snow leopard feces collected in northern Pakistan also contained remains of <a href="/wiki/Rhesus_macaque" title="Rhesus macaque">rhesus macaque</a> (<i>Macaca mulatta</i>), <a href="/wiki/Masked_palm_civet" title="Masked palm civet">masked palm civet</a> (<i>Paguma larvata</i>), <a href="/wiki/Cape_hare" title="Cape hare">Cape hare</a> (<i>Lepus capensis</i>), <a href="/wiki/House_mouse" title="House mouse">house mouse</a> (<i>Mus musculus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Kashmir_field_mouse" title="Kashmir field mouse">Kashmir field mouse</a> (<i>Apodemus rusiges</i>), <a href="/wiki/Grey_dwarf_hamster" title="Grey dwarf hamster">grey dwarf hamster</a> (<i>Cricetulus migratorius</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Turkestan_rat" title="Turkestan rat">Turkestan rat</a> (<i>Rattus pyctoris</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, a snow leopard was photographed carrying a freshly killed <a href="/wiki/Woolly_flying_squirrel" class="mw-redirect" title="Woolly flying squirrel">woolly flying squirrel</a> (<i>Eupetaurus cinereus</i>) near <a href="/wiki/Gangotri_National_Park" title="Gangotri National Park">Gangotri National Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In Mongolia, <a href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic sheep">domestic sheep</a> comprises less than 20% of its diet, although wild prey has been reduced and interactions with people are common.<sup id="cite_ref-Shehzad2012_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shehzad2012-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Snow leopards actively pursue prey down steep mountainsides, using the momentum of their initial leap to chase animals for up to 300&#160;m (980&#160;ft). They drag the prey to a safe location and consume all edible parts of the carcass. They can survive on a single Himalayan blue sheep for two weeks before hunting again, and one adult individual apparently needs 20–30 adult blue sheep per year.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WCoW_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Snow leopards have been recorded to hunt successfully in pairs, especially mating pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-hunt_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hunt-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The snow leopard is capable of killing most animals in its range, with the probable exception of the adult male <a href="/wiki/Wild_yak" title="Wild yak">yak</a>. It also eats a significant amount of vegetation, including grass and twigs. It has not been reported to attack humans, is easily driven away from livestock and readily abandons kills, often without defending itself.<sup id="cite_ref-WCoW_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_life_cycle">Reproduction and life cycle</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:SnowCubs01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/SnowCubs01.jpg/200px-SnowCubs01.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/SnowCubs01.jpg/300px-SnowCubs01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/SnowCubs01.jpg/400px-SnowCubs01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2144" data-file-height="1424" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Cubs at the Cat Survival Trust in <a href="/wiki/Welwyn" title="Welwyn">Welwyn</a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Schneeleoparden_Kailash_und_Dshamilja_frontal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Schneeleoparden_Kailash_und_Dshamilja_frontal.jpg/200px-Schneeleoparden_Kailash_und_Dshamilja_frontal.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="164" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Schneeleoparden_Kailash_und_Dshamilja_frontal.jpg/300px-Schneeleoparden_Kailash_und_Dshamilja_frontal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Schneeleoparden_Kailash_und_Dshamilja_frontal.jpg/400px-Schneeleoparden_Kailash_und_Dshamilja_frontal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2218" data-file-height="1814" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Female with her cub in <a href="/wiki/Zurich_Zoo" class="mw-redirect" title="Zurich Zoo">Zurich Zoo</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Snow leopards become sexually mature at two to three years, and normally live for 15–18 years in the wild. In captivity they can live for up to 25 years. <a href="/wiki/Oestrus" class="mw-redirect" title="Oestrus">Oestrus</a> typically lasts five to eight days, and males tend not to seek out another partner after mating, probably because the short mating season does not allow sufficient time. Paired snow leopards mate in <a href="/wiki/Lordosis_behavior" title="Lordosis behavior">the usual felid posture</a>, from 12 to 36 times a day. They are unusual among large cats in that they have a well-defined birth peak. They usually <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">mate</a> in late winter, marked by a noticeable increase in marking and calling. Females have a <a href="/wiki/Gestation_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Gestation period">gestation period</a> of 90–100 days, and the cubs are born between April and June.<sup id="cite_ref-WCoW_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> 
A <a href="/wiki/Litter_(animal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Litter (animal)">litter</a> usually consists of two to three cubs, in exceptional cases there can be up to seven.<sup id="cite_ref-Heptner1972_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heptner1972-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The female gives birth in a rocky den or crevice lined with fur shed from her underside. The cubs are born blind and helpless, although already with a thick coat of fur, and weigh 320 to 567&#160;g (11.3 to 20.0&#160;oz). Their eyes open at around seven days, and the cubs can walk at five weeks and are fully weaned by 10 weeks. The cubs leave the den when they are around two to four months of age.<sup id="cite_ref-WCoW_27-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Three radio-collared snow leopards in Mongolia's Tost Mountains gave birth between late April and late June. Two female cubs started to part from their mothers at the age of 20 to 21 months, but reunited with them several times for a few days over a period of 4–7 months. One male cub separated from its mother at the age of about 22 months, but stayed in her vicinity for a month and moved out of his natal range at 23 months of age.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The snow leopard has a <a href="/wiki/Generation_time" title="Generation time">generation length</a> of eight years.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Threats">Threats</span></h2>
<p>Major threats to the population includes poaching and illegal trade of its skins and body parts.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Between 1999 and 2002, three live snow leopard cubs and 16 skins were <a href="/wiki/Confiscated" class="mw-redirect" title="Confiscated">confiscated</a>, 330 <a href="/wiki/Trapping" title="Trapping">traps</a> were destroyed and 110 poachers were arrested in Kyrgyzstan. <a href="/wiki/Undercover_operation" class="mw-redirect" title="Undercover operation">Undercover operations</a> in the country revealed an illegal trade network with links to Russia and China via Kazakhstan. The major skin trade center in the region is the city of <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dexel2002_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dexel2002-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In Tibet and Mongolia, skins are used for traditional dresses, and meat in <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Tibetan_medicine" title="Traditional Tibetan medicine">traditional Tibetan medicine</a> to cure <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidney</a> problems; bones are used in <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Mongolian_medicine" title="Traditional Mongolian medicine">Mongolian medicine</a> for treating <a href="/wiki/Rheumatism" title="Rheumatism">rheumatism</a>, injuries and pain of human bones and <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">tendons</a>. Between 1996 and 2002, 37 skins were found in wildlife markets and tourist shops in Mongolia.<sup id="cite_ref-Theile2003_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Theile2003-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Between 2003 and 2016, 710 skins were traded, of which 288 skins were confiscated. In China, an estimated 103 to 236 animals are poached every year, in Mongolia between 34 and 53, in Pakistan between 23 and 53, in India from 21 to 45, and in Tajikistan 20 to 25. In 2016, a survey of Chinese websites revealed 15 advertisements for 44 snow leopard products; the dealers offered skins, canine teeth, claws and a tongue.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In September 2014, nine snow leopard skins were found during a market survey in Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-Maheshwari_al2016_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maheshwari_al2016-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">Greenhouse gas</a> emissions will likely cause a shift of the <a href="/wiki/Treeline" class="mw-redirect" title="Treeline">treeline</a> in the Himalayas and a shrinking of the <a href="/wiki/Alpine_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpine zone">alpine zone</a>, which may reduce snow leopard habitat by an estimate 30%.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Where snow leopards prey on domestic <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>, they are subject to <a href="/wiki/Human%E2%80%93wildlife_conflict" title="Human–wildlife conflict">human–wildlife conflict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> 
The loss of natural prey due to overgrazing by livestock, poaching, and defense of livestock are the major drivers for the ever decreasing snow leopard population.<sup id="cite_ref-WCoW_27-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCoW-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Livestock also cause habitat degradation, which, alongside the increasing use of forests for fuel, reduces snow leopard habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<td>30–120<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The snow leopard is listed in <a href="/wiki/CITES_Appendix_I" class="mw-redirect" title="CITES Appendix I">CITES Appendix I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowell&amp;Jackson1996_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowell&amp;Jackson1996-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> It has been listed as threatened with extinction in Schedule I of the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Conservation_of_Migratory_Species_of_Wild_Animals" title="Convention on the Conservation of Migratory Species of Wild Animals">Convention on the Conservation of Migratory Species of Wild Animals</a> since 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-Theile2003_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Theile2003-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
Hunting snow leopards has been prohibited in Kyrgyzstan since the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-Dexel2002_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dexel2002-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In India, the snow leopard is granted the highest level of protection under the <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_Protection_Act,_1972" class="mw-redirect" title="Wildlife Protection Act, 1972">Wildlife Protection Act, 1972</a>, and hunting is sentenced with <a href="/wiki/Imprisonment" title="Imprisonment">imprisonment</a> of 3–7 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Bhatnagar_al2016_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bhatnagar_al2016-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In Nepal, it has been legally protected since 1973, with penalties of 5–15 years in prison and a <a href="/wiki/Fine_(penalty)" title="Fine (penalty)">fine</a> for poaching and trading it.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
Since 1978, it has been listed in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>’s Red Book and is still inscribed today in the <a href="/wiki/Red_Data_Book_of_the_Russian_Federation" title="Red Data Book of the Russian Federation">Red Data Book of the Russian Federation</a> as threatened with extinction. Hunting snow leopards is only permitted for the purposes of conservation and monitoring, and to eliminate a threat to the life of humans and livestock. <a href="/wiki/Smuggling" title="Smuggling">Smuggling</a> of snow leopard body parts is punished with imprisonment and a <a href="/wiki/Fine_(penalty)" title="Fine (penalty)">fine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
Hunting snow leopards has been prohibited in Afghanistan since 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-Maheshwari_al2016_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maheshwari_al2016-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
In China, it has been protected by law since 1989; hunting and trading snow leopards or their body parts constitute a <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">criminal</a> offence that is punishable by the <a href="/wiki/Confiscation" title="Confiscation">confiscation</a> of property, a fine and a <a href="/wiki/Sentence_(law)" title="Sentence (law)">sentence</a> of at least 10 years in prison.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
It has been protected in Bhutan since 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-Lham_al2016_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lham_al2016-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of 2020, 35 cameras were installed on the outskirts of Almaty, Kazakhstan in hopes to catch footage of snow leopards. In November 2021, it was announced by the Russian World Wildlife Fund (WWF) that snow leopards were spotted 65 times on these cameras in the Trans-Ili Alatau mountains since the cameras were installed.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Snow leopards inhabit the following <a href="/wiki/Protected_area" title="Protected area">protected areas</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>in Kazakhstan: <a href="/wiki/Aksu-Zhabagly_Nature_Reserve" title="Aksu-Zhabagly Nature Reserve">Aksu-Zhabagly Nature Reserve</a><sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in Russia: <a href="/wiki/Katun_Nature_Reserve" title="Katun Nature Reserve">Katun Nature Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sayano-Shushenski_Nature_Reserve" title="Sayano-Shushenski Nature Reserve">Sayano-Shushenski Nature Reserve</a><sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in Kyrgyzstan: <a href="/wiki/Sarychat-Ertash_State_Nature_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarychat-Ertash State Nature Reserve">Sarychat-Ertash State Nature Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sary-Chelek_Nature_Reserve" title="Sary-Chelek Nature Reserve">Sary-Chelek Nature Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Besh-Tash_State_Nature_National_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Besh-Tash State Nature National Park">Besh-Tash State Nature National Park</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kyrgyz-Ata_National_Park&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kyrgyz-Ata National Park (page does not exist)">Kyrgyz-Ata National Park</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Karakol_National_Park&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Karakol National Park (page does not exist)">Karakol National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chychkan_Wildlife_Refuge" class="mw-redirect" title="Chychkan Wildlife Refuge">Chychkan Wildlife Refuge</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in Uzbekistan: <a href="/wiki/Chatkalskiy_State_Nature_Reserve" title="Chatkalskiy State Nature Reserve">Chatkalskiy State Nature Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaamin_National_Park" title="Zaamin National Park">Zaamin National Park</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ugam-Chatkal_National_Park&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ugam-Chatkal National Park (page does not exist)">Ugam-Chatkal National Park</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hissar_National_Reserve&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hissar National Reserve (page does not exist)">Hissar National Reserve</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in Tajikistan: <a href="/wiki/Pamir_National_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Pamir National Park">Pamir National Park</a><sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in Mongolia: <a href="/wiki/Altai_Tavan_Bogd_National_Park" title="Altai Tavan Bogd National Park">Altai Tavan Bogd National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tsambagarav_Uul_National_Park" title="Tsambagarav Uul National Park">Tsambagarav Uul National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Har_Us_Nuur_National_Park" title="Har Us Nuur National Park">Har Us Nuur National Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Gurvansaikhan_National_Park" title="Gobi Gurvansaikhan National Park">Gobi Gurvansaikhan National Park</a><sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in China: <a href="/wiki/Chang_Tang_Nature_Reserve" title="Chang Tang Nature Reserve">Chang Tang Nature Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qomolangma_National_Nature_Preserve" title="Qomolangma National Nature Preserve">Qomolangma National Nature Preserve</a><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sanjiangyuan" title="Sanjiangyuan">Sanjiangyuan</a> National Nature Reserve on the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tomur_National_Conservation_Zone&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tomur National Conservation Zone (page does not exist)">Tomur National Conservation Zone</a> in the western <a href="/wiki/Tianshan_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Tianshan Mountains">Tianshan Mountains</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Qilianshan National Nature Reserve in the <a href="/wiki/Qilian_Mountains" title="Qilian Mountains">Qilian Mountains</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in Pakistan: <a href="/wiki/Chitral_National_Park" title="Chitral National Park">Chitral National Park</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa" class="mw-redirect" title="Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa">Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa</a> region, <a href="/wiki/Central_Karakoram_National_Park" title="Central Karakoram National Park">Central Karakoram National Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khunjerab_National_Park" title="Khunjerab National Park">Khunjerab National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gilgit-Baltistan" title="Gilgit-Baltistan">Gilgit-Baltistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deosai_National_Park" title="Deosai National Park">Deosai National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naltar_Wildlife_Sanctuary" title="Naltar Wildlife Sanctuary">Naltar Wildlife Sanctuary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baltistan_Wildlife_Sanctuary" title="Baltistan Wildlife Sanctuary">Baltistan Wildlife Sanctuary</a> and several protected areas that are smaller than 300&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (120&#160;sq&#160;mi);<sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in India: Hemis National Park, <a href="/wiki/Kishtwar_National_Park" title="Kishtwar National Park">Kishtwar National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dachigam_National_Park" title="Dachigam National Park">Dachigam National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulmarg_Wildlife_Sanctuary" title="Gulmarg Wildlife Sanctuary">Gulmarg Wildlife Sanctuary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hirpora_Wildlife_Sanctuary" title="Hirpora Wildlife Sanctuary">Hirpora Wildlife Sanctuary</a>, Rangdum Wildlife Reserve, Overa-Aru, Kanji, Gya-Miru and Baltal-Thajwas Wildlife Sanctuaries in <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir_(union_territory)" title="Jammu and Kashmir (union territory)">Jammu and Kashmir</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pin_Valley_National_Park" title="Pin Valley National Park">Pin Valley National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Himalayan_National_Park" title="Great Himalayan National Park">Great Himalayan National Park</a>, Rupi-Bhaba Wildlife Sanctuary, Sechu Tuan Nala Wildlife Sanctuary<sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and Kibber Wildlife Sanctuary in Himachal Pradesh;<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nanda_Devi_National_Park" title="Nanda Devi National Park">Nanda Devi National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gangotri_National_Park" title="Gangotri National Park">Gangotri National Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_Flowers" class="mw-redirect" title="Valley of Flowers">Valley of Flowers</a> National Park in <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a>; <a href="/wiki/Khangchendzonga_National_Park" title="Khangchendzonga National Park">Khangchendzonga National Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dibang_Wildlife_Sanctuary" title="Dibang Wildlife Sanctuary">Dibang Wildlife Sanctuary</a> in the Eastern Himalayas;<sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in Nepal: <a href="/wiki/Api_Nampa_Conservation_Area" title="Api Nampa Conservation Area">Api Nampa Conservation Area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhorpatan_Hunting_Reserve" title="Dhorpatan Hunting Reserve">Dhorpatan Hunting Reserve</a>, Shey Phoksundo National Park, <a href="/wiki/Annapurna_Conservation_Area" title="Annapurna Conservation Area">Annapurna Conservation Area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manaslu_Conservation_Area" title="Manaslu Conservation Area">Manaslu Conservation Area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Langtang_National_Park" title="Langtang National Park">Langtang National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sagarmatha_National_Park" title="Sagarmatha National Park">Sagarmatha National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makalu_Barun_National_Park" title="Makalu Barun National Park">Makalu Barun National Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kanchenjunga_Conservation_Area" title="Kanchenjunga Conservation Area">Kanchenjunga Conservation Area</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>in Bhutan: <a href="/wiki/Bumdeling_Wildlife_Sanctuary" title="Bumdeling Wildlife Sanctuary">Bumdeling Wildlife Sanctuary</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Johansson2016_40-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johansson2016-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jigme_Dorji_National_Park" title="Jigme Dorji National Park">Jigme Dorji National Park</a><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Wangchuck_Centennial_National_Park" title="Wangchuck Centennial National Park">Wangchuck Centennial National Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_Snow_Leopard_Forum">Global Snow Leopard Forum</span></h3>
<p>In 2013, government leaders and officials from all 12 countries encompassing the snow leopard's range (Afghanistan, Bhutan, China, India, Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, Mongolia, Nepal, Pakistan, Russia, Tajikistan, and Uzbekistan) came together at the Global Snow Leopard Forum (GSLF) initiated by the then-President of Kyrgyzstan <a href="/wiki/Almazbek_Atambayev" title="Almazbek Atambayev">Almazbek Atambayev</a>, and the State Agency on Environmental Protection and Forestry under the government of Kyrgyzstan. The meeting was held in <a href="/wiki/Bishkek" title="Bishkek">Bishkek</a>, and all countries agreed that the snow leopard and the high mountain habitat need trans-boundary support to ensure a viable future for snow leopard populations, and to safeguard its fragile environment. The event brought together many partners, including <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-governmental organisation">NGOs</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Snow_Leopard_Conservancy" title="Snow Leopard Conservancy">Snow Leopard Conservancy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Snow_Leopard_Trust" title="Snow Leopard Trust">Snow Leopard Trust</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nature_and_Biodiversity_Conservation_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Nature and Biodiversity Conservation Union">Nature and Biodiversity Conservation Union</a>. Also supporting the initiative were the Snow Leopard Network, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Global_Tiger_Initiative" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Tiger Initiative">Global Tiger Initiative</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">United Nations Development Programme</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Wild_Fund_for_Nature" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wild Fund for Nature">World Wild Fund for Nature</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">United States Agency for International Development</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Global_Environment_Facility" title="Global Environment Facility">Global Environment Facility</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the GSLF meeting, the 12 range countries signed the Bishkek Declaration, which stated: "[We] acknowledge that the snow leopard is an irreplaceable symbol of our nations' natural and cultural heritage and an indicator of the health and sustainability of mountain ecosystems; and we recognize that mountain ecosystems inhabited by snow leopards provide essential <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a>, including storing and releasing water from the origins of river systems benefitting one-third of the world’s human population; sustaining the pastoral and agricultural livelihoods of local communities which depend on biodiversity for food, fuel, fodder, and medicine; and offering inspiration, recreation, and economic opportunities."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_captivity">In captivity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lightmatter_snowleopard.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Lightmatter_snowleopard.jpg/220px-Lightmatter_snowleopard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Lightmatter_snowleopard.jpg/330px-Lightmatter_snowleopard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Lightmatter_snowleopard.jpg/440px-Lightmatter_snowleopard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lightmatter_snowleopard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Snow leopard in the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Zoo" title="San Diego Zoo">San Diego Zoo</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Zoo" title="Moscow Zoo">Moscow Zoo</a> exhibited the first captive snow leopard in 1872 that had been caught in <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a>. In Kyrgyzstan, 420 live snow leopards were caught between 1936 and 1988 and exported to zoos around the world. The <a href="/wiki/Bronx_Zoo" title="Bronx Zoo">Bronx Zoo</a> housed a live snow leopard in 1903; this was the first ever specimen exhibited in a North American zoo.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Captive_breeding" title="Captive breeding">captive bred</a> snow leopard cubs were born in the 1990s in the <a href="/wiki/Beijing_Zoo" title="Beijing Zoo">Beijing Zoo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dexel2002_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dexel2002-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
The Snow Leopard <a href="/wiki/Species_Survival_Plan" title="Species Survival Plan">Species Survival Plan</a> was initiated in 1984; by 1986, <a href="/wiki/List_of_zoos_in_the_United_States" title="List of zoos in the United States">American zoos</a> held 234 individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SnowLeopard10000KZT.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/SnowLeopard10000KZT.jpg/220px-SnowLeopard10000KZT.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/SnowLeopard10000KZT.jpg/330px-SnowLeopard10000KZT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/SnowLeopard10000KZT.jpg/440px-SnowLeopard10000KZT.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SnowLeopard10000KZT.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Snow leopard on the reverse of the old 10,000-Kazakhstani tenge banknote</div></div></div>
<p>The snow leopard is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldry</a> and as an emblem in Central Asia. It has long been used as a political symbol, the <i><a href="/wiki/Aq_Bars" title="Aq Bars">Aq Bars</a></i> ('White Leopard'), by <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakhs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">Bulgars</a>. A snow leopard is depicted on the official seal of <a href="/wiki/Almaty" title="Almaty">Almaty</a> and on the former 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstani_tenge" title="Kazakhstani tenge">Kazakhstani tenge</a> banknote. A mythical winged <i>Aq Bars</i> is depicted on the national <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Tatarstan" class="mw-redirect" title="Coat of arms of Tatarstan">coat of arms of Tatarstan</a>, the seal of the city of <a href="/wiki/Samarqand" class="mw-redirect" title="Samarqand">Samarqand</a>, Uzbekistan and the old coat of arms of <a href="/wiki/Nur-Sultan" class="mw-redirect" title="Nur-Sultan">Nur-Sultan</a>. In Kyrgyzstan, it has been used in highly stylized form in the modern emblem of the capital <a href="/wiki/Bishkek" title="Bishkek">Bishkek</a>, and the same art has been integrated into the badge of the <a href="/wiki/Scouting_in_Kyrgyzstan#Kyrgyzstan_Girl_Scouts_Association" title="Scouting in Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan Girl Scouts Association</a>. It is also considered to be a sacred creature by the <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_people" title="Kyrgyz people">Kyrgyz people</a>. A crowned snow leopard features in the arms of <a href="/wiki/Shushensky_District" title="Shushensky District">Shushensky District</a> in Russia. It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Indian_state_animals" title="List of Indian state animals">state animal</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a> in India.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
